# How to Start a Business in Dubai

### The Dubai Syndicate Way

**The Practical Field Guide to 35 Industries**

*From Restaurant to Real Estate, Salon to SaaS*


*Book Two — working draft.*

---



# Part One — Foundations

*What every Dubai business shares — the city, the jurisdiction, the licence, the money, and the mindset.*

---



PART ONE — FOUNDATIONS

# Chapter 1 · Why Dubai

*The case for the city — made honestly, with the limits drawn in.*

---

## The question worth asking

Nearly everyone who sits across from me starts with the same question: *Can I make money in Dubai?* It is an understandable question, and it has a disappointing answer — yes. People make money here every day, in almost every industry in this book. But that answer is useless to you, because it is equally true that people lose money here every day, in those same industries. The question does not separate the two outcomes. It cannot tell you anything you can act on.

The question that can is narrower and less comfortable: *Does my business — with the capital I actually have, the time I can actually give it, and the temperament I actually possess — fit this city?* That is the question this book exists to answer, thirty-five times over, once for each industry in Part Two. But before we reach any industry, we have to be honest about the city itself. If Dubai is wrong for you, no chapter in Part Two will fix that. And if it is right for you, you should know precisely why — not because a video told you so.

## What Dubai actually is

Dubai is one of seven emirates that make up the United Arab Emirates. It is home to more than three and a half million residents, of whom roughly nine in ten were born somewhere else. Hold that figure in mind, because it shapes everything that follows: this is a city of arrivals. Almost everyone you will sell to, hire, partner with, or compete against came here on purpose, to work.

That is not an accident of history; it is the result of design. Dubai has comparatively little oil — far less than its neighbours — and it understood that early. So it built itself, deliberately, as a marketplace. First as a re-export hub, moving goods between continents. Then as a destination, drawing close to eighteen million international visitors a year. Then as a centre for finance, logistics, and professional services. The city sits at GMT+4, which means a single working day here overlaps the morning in East Asia and the afternoon in Europe. Jebel Ali is one of the largest ports on earth. Dubai International is among the busiest airports in the world for international passengers. None of this happened by luck.

It still doesn't. The government treats company formation, licensing, and visas the way other cities treat roads and water — as infrastructure. The current policy, the Dubai Economic Agenda known as D33, sets the explicit goal of doubling the size of the city's economy within a decade. You may find that ambition reassuring or excessive, but you should not ignore it, because it tells you what kind of environment you are entering: one that *wants* more businesses, and competes globally to attract them. That is the wind at your back. The rest of this chapter is about the parts of the journey the wind does not cover.

## The case, stated plainly

Here, then, is the genuine case for Dubai — six advantages that are real, each paired with the limit you should not forget.

**1. Location and logistics.** The three-continent working day is not a slogan; it is a practical asset if your business touches trade, distribution, or cross-border services. Goods clear quickly. Suppliers are close. Clients in three time zones are reachable before lunch. The limit: location helps a trading company or a consultancy enormously. It does very little for a neighbourhood salon, whose market is the four streets around it. Know which kind of business you are building.

**2. Real ownership.** For most of Dubai's history, a foreign founder on the mainland needed an Emirati partner holding a majority share. That requirement was removed for the large majority of commercial and industrial activities in 2021. In the free zones, full foreign ownership was always the rule. In plain terms: you can, today, own your company outright. That changes how you should think about control, profit, and succession — and we will return to it properly in Chapter 2.

**3. The tax position — stated accurately.** This is where honesty matters most, because the myth is loud. There is no personal income tax in the UAE; what you draw as salary or dividend is not taxed at the personal level. But "tax-free" as a description of *doing business* is no longer accurate. A value-added tax of five per cent has applied since 2018. A federal corporate tax of nine per cent has applied since June 2023 on business profits above AED 375,000 — profits below that threshold are still taxed at zero. Qualifying free zone companies can, under specific conditions, retain a zero rate on qualifying income. The honest summary is this: the tax burden in Dubai remains among the lowest of any serious commercial city in the world, but it is not zero, and a business plan that depends on it being zero is already wrong. Build your numbers on the real rates.

**4. The customer base.** Dubai's population is young, working, and — in meaningful pockets — willing to spend. Add the visitors, and you have a market far larger than three and a half million. The limit, and it is a real one: a city of arrivals is also a city of departures. Customers leave. Loyalty is shorter here than in a settled market. You will re-earn your customer base more often than you would elsewhere, and you must price and plan for that turnover rather than be surprised by it.

**5. Speed.** You can form a company in Dubai in days. Banking, premises, talent, payment systems, and suppliers are all available and competitive. For a founder, that speed is a gift — it shortens the distance between decision and trading. The limit is simply that the same speed is available to everyone, including the person who wants to copy you. Easy entry means crowded markets. Speed is an advantage only if you also have something the next entrant does not.

**6. Stability.** The UAE is politically stable and physically safe, and the dirham has been pegged to the US dollar for decades at a fixed rate. For a business owner, predictability is not a soft benefit. It means you can plan in a straight line — your costs, your pricing, and your currency exposure do not lurch from one quarter to the next. Many places in the world cannot offer that. Dubai can.

## The myths that cost people money

Against those six real advantages sit four beliefs that are not true, and that cost people money in a predictable order.

**The first myth is that Dubai is tax-free.** We have already dealt with the facts. The damage the myth does is subtler than a wrong number on a spreadsheet: it sets an expectation of effortlessness. Founders who arrive believing the city will cost them nothing are the same founders who are unprepared for the fees that appear at every step — licence renewals, visa costs, the establishment card, attestations, deposits. None of these is large on its own. Together, unbudgeted, they drain a launch.

**The second myth is that setup is the hard part.** It is the opposite. Setup is a transaction — you pay, you file, you receive a licence. It is the most certain part of the entire journey, which is exactly why so many founders over-invest in it. They spend the whole budget on a licence and a beautiful fit-out, open the doors, and discover they have no money left for the eighteen months in which the business is supposed to actually find its feet. The licence is the start of the work, not the proof of it.

**The third myth is that the city will bring you customers.** Dubai brings footfall to *Dubai*. It fills the airport and the malls and the calendar of events. It does not walk anyone through your door. That is your job, and it is hard enough that every single industry chapter in Part Two devotes a dedicated section to it, titled "First three customers." If a business cannot answer the question of where its first three customers come from — by name, by channel, by date — it does not yet have a business. It has a licence.

**The fourth myth is that everyone in Dubai is rich.** The Dubai of supercars and weekend brunches is real, but it is a thin and highly visible surface. The economy underneath it is built, like every economy, on small and medium businesses running on disciplined margins, and on a working population that budgets carefully and watches prices closely. If you set your prices for the Dubai of the postcard, you will price yourself out of the Dubai that actually shows up. Serve the city as it is.

## The real math

So let us put real numbers on the table, because the rest of this book runs on them.

A basic free zone licence can be advertised from around AED 12,500 a year. Take that figure and treat it as the smallest line in your launch budget, not the headline. The true cost of opening includes residence visas for you and any staff, an establishment card, medical tests and Emirates ID, premises or at least a flexi-desk, a security deposit, insurance, and — the line founders most often omit — working capital. By the time the doors open, the licence is frequently a fraction of what has been spent. A reliable instinct: whatever the licence quote, assume the genuine first-year cost is several times larger, and confirm the specifics for your industry using the figures in each Part Two chapter and the directory in Part Three.

Then there is the number nobody enjoys: runway. Almost no business in this book pays you back in its first month. Most take six to twelve months to reach a steady footing. A cash runway that covers both the business and your own living costs across that period is not pessimism — it is the plan. A founder with a twelve-month business and three months of money does not have a cash-flow problem somewhere in the future; they have one already, on day one.

Finally, the cash cycle — the rhythm of money in and money out — which matters so much that every Part Two chapter analyses it directly. Dubai has a particular trap here. You may be required to pay your rent annually, in advance, in one or two cheques. Meanwhile, if you sell to other businesses, you may wait sixty or ninety days to be paid. A profitable business can run out of cash in the gap between those two facts. It has happened to more Dubai businesses than bad ideas ever have. Profit is an opinion; cash is a fact. Manage the fact.

## Who Dubai is for — and who it isn't

This brings us to the only verdict that matters in this chapter.

Dubai is for the founder who arrives with a real runway, with a product or service the city genuinely wants, with the temperament to operate in a fast, crowded, and properly regulated market, and with the patience to do the unglamorous work — the licensing, the bookkeeping, the slow accumulation of customers — for longer than feels comfortable. Dubai rewards operators. If you want to build something and run it well, the city is, with few qualifications, on your side.

Dubai is not *yet* for the founder whose entire plan rests on the word "tax-free"; for the founder with three months of money and a twelve-month business; for the founder who expects income to be passive from the first week; or for the founder who has not tested whether anyone actually wants what they intend to sell. Note the word "yet." None of these is a permanent disqualification. Each is a sequencing problem. Extend the runway. Validate the demand. Correct the expectation. Then come. The city will still be here, and it will be a far better bet for the founder who arrives in that order.

## How to read the rest of this book

A short word on what you are holding, so you can use it efficiently.

**Part One** — these five chapters — covers the foundations every Dubai business shares, regardless of industry: the city itself, the jurisdiction decision, licensing, money, and the operating mindset. Read it in order. Each chapter assumes the one before it.

**Part Two** is the field guide and the heart of the book: thirty-five industries, six pages each, organised into eight categories — Food and Beverage, Personal and Lifestyle Services, Home and Property Services, Mobility and Transport, Trade and Commerce, Tourism and Events, Professional Services, and Tech and Specialised. Every one of the thirty-five chapters is built on the identical eight-section template, so you can compare any two industries directly: (1) what the business actually is in Dubai, (2) the capital required, (3) the licence and jurisdiction, (4) the first three customers, (5) the profit margins and cash cycle, (6) the top three mistakes, (7) who is already winning, and (8) the verdict. Read the categories that interest you; use the rest as a reference.

**Part Three** is the resource section — the directories, contacts, and checklists that turn a decision into an application.

One closing thought to carry into Chapter 2. Dubai is not a lottery ticket, and it was never built to be one. It is a well-engineered marketplace that pays out, reliably, to people who arrive prepared. Everything in this book is in the service of that one word — *prepared*. We begin with the decision that shapes every other one you will make, the choice that sets your costs, your ownership, your customers, and your licence in a single stroke: where, in legal terms, your business will live. That is Chapter 2.



PART ONE — FOUNDATIONS

# Chapter 2 · The jurisdiction decision

*Mainland, free zone, or offshore — the choice that sets your costs, your reach, and your customers.*

---

## The decision under the decision

Most founders believe their first real choice is *what* business to start. It is not. By the time you are reading this book you almost certainly have a business in mind, or a short list. The first choice that genuinely shapes your outcome is quieter and more technical, and it is the one this chapter exists to settle: *where, in legal terms, will your company live?*

In Dubai, "where" does not mean an address. It means a jurisdiction — a legal home that determines who may own your company, where you are permitted to trade, what your licence costs, how many visas you may sponsor, what premises you must hold, how a bank will read your file, and whether you must be audited. Three broad options exist: the **mainland**, the **free zones**, and **offshore**. They are not three prices for the same thing. They are three different legal environments, and choosing the wrong one is not a saving — it is a structural error you will spend money and months unwinding.

I have watched more launches damaged by this single decision than by any other. Almost always the damage traces to the same cause: the founder chose the cheapest package on a comparison website instead of choosing the jurisdiction that matched their customers. So before any number is discussed, hold on to one sentence. *The right jurisdiction is the one your customers can legally and comfortably buy from.* Everything below is the detail behind that sentence.

## The mainland

A mainland company is licensed by the **Dubai Department of Economy and Tourism** — the DET, formerly the DED. It is the "onshore" UAE company, the default for any business whose market is the people and companies of Dubai itself.

**Ownership.** This is the reform that changed the landscape. For most of Dubai's history a foreign founder on the mainland needed an Emirati partner holding 51 per cent of the shares. Since 2021, 100 per cent foreign ownership is permitted for the large majority of commercial and industrial activities. A limited set of strategic activities still carries local-ownership or local-agent requirements, so you must confirm the position for your specific activity — but for the typical restaurant, agency, trading company, or salon, you can now own the business outright.

**Where you may trade — the point that matters most.** A mainland licence lets you trade anywhere: with the public, with other businesses, across all seven emirates, and in government tenders. You can hold a shop on a main road, sell directly to walk-in customers, and invoice a government department. There is no internal wall between you and the Dubai market. For a business whose customers *are* the Dubai mainland, this freedom is not a luxury — it is the whole point.

**Cost shape, premises, and visas.** The mainland asks more of you. You will generally need a physical office or unit with a registered **Ejari** tenancy contract, and your visa quota is tied broadly to the size of that space. Expect first-year costs to run higher than a basic free zone package — licence, registered premises, and approvals together. In return you receive unrestricted market access and a visa allocation that scales with real premises.

**Banks and audit.** Banks tend to read a mainland company with a genuine office and a clear local market as straightforward to understand, which helps — though, as Chapter 4 will make plain, no account is ever simple to open. Audited financial statements are increasingly expected, and corporate-tax compliance makes proper books non-negotiable regardless.

## The free zones

Dubai has more than forty free zones, each a designated economic area run by its own authority with its own licence regime, its own activity list, and its own pricing. DMCC, IFZA, Meydan, Dubai South, DIFC, Dubai CommerCity, Dubai Internet City — these are not branches of one system. They are separate jurisdictions, and they differ from one another almost as much as they differ from the mainland.

**Ownership.** Simple and unchanged: 100 per cent foreign ownership has always been the rule in the free zones. That was their original promise, and it remains true.

**Where you may trade — read this twice.** A free zone company may trade freely *within its own free zone, with other free zones, and internationally*. What it generally may **not** do is sell directly into the Dubai mainland market as if it held a mainland licence. To reach mainland customers, a free zone company typically works through a mainland distributor, appoints a commercial agent, or opens a mainland branch — each an added cost and an added step. This is the single most expensive misunderstanding in Dubai company formation. A free zone licence does not entitle you to a shopfront on a mainland street, nor to sell directly to the mainland public. If your customers are the Dubai mainland, a free zone licence puts a wall between you and them, and you will pay — in margin, in agents, in branches — to get around the wall you chose to build.

**Cost shape, premises, and visas.** This is where free zones are genuinely attractive. A basic licence is advertised from roughly AED 12,500 a year, and many zones offer a **flexi-desk** — a shared or notional workspace that satisfies the premises requirement without a full office lease. Visa quotas are usually packaged with the licence, often a fixed small number of visas, and you can frequently buy more. For a consultancy, a trading company selling internationally, an e-commerce business, or a tech firm with no need for mainland walk-in trade, this lean cost shape is a real advantage. Treat the headline figure honestly, though — as Chapter 1 set out, the true first-year cost is a multiple of the licence once visas, the establishment card, Emirates ID, deposits, insurance, and working capital are counted.

**Banks and audit.** Banks have grown cautious about free zone companies that show a flexi-desk, no local market, and thin substance — they ask harder questions about what the business actually does and where its money comes from. A free zone company with real activity and real documentation is bankable; a shell-shaped one is not. Audit requirements vary by zone — some mandate audited accounts, some do not — and the corporate-tax regime, including the qualifying free zone person rules, makes proper accounts essential in any case.

## Offshore

The third option is the one most often misunderstood, because the word "offshore" carries baggage from elsewhere. In the UAE an **offshore company** — such as a JAFZA Offshore or RAK ICC company — is a specific, legitimate tool with a narrow purpose.

An offshore company is for **holding assets, owning intellectual property, and international structuring**. It can hold shares in other companies, own property where permitted, and hold a bank account. What it cannot do is operate inside the UAE: it has no licence to trade in the local market, and — this is decisive for most readers of this book — **it does not grant residence visas**. You cannot run a Dubai restaurant, agency, or shop through an offshore company, and you cannot get a residence visa through one.

So offshore is not an alternative to the mainland or a free zone for an operating business. It is a different instrument for a different job — a holding layer, a structuring vehicle. If your goal is to *operate* a business in Dubai and *live* here while you do, offshore is not your answer, and any adviser who offers it as the cheap route to a trading company is steering you wrong.

## How to decide

Now the method. The decision is not made by comparing prices. It is made by answering three questions honestly, in order.

**First: who are your customers, and where are they?** This question decides almost everything. If your customers are the Dubai mainland public or mainland businesses — a café, a salon, a clinic, a retail shop, a maintenance company, a firm chasing government contracts — you need a **mainland** licence. The free zone discount is no discount at all if it walls you off from the people you intend to sell to. If your customers are international, or other free zone companies, or you are running an online or consultancy business that never needs a mainland shopfront — a **free zone** licence will serve you well and cost you less. If you are not selling at all, but holding or structuring — that is the narrow case for **offshore**.

**Second: does the jurisdiction's activity list actually fit your business?** Every free zone licenses a defined list of activities, and the lists differ. A zone built for media will not cleanly license a food-trading company; a zone built for commodities may not fit a design studio. Before you commit, confirm that your exact activity — and every activity you expect to add later — is available in that jurisdiction. Founders who skip this step discover, a year in, that the natural next step for their business is not permitted on their licence.

**Third: what do your premises and visas genuinely require?** If you need to sponsor a team of fifteen, a flexi-desk will not carry that quota and a free zone may cap you below it; a mainland office sized to your headcount will. If you are a solo consultant who will never have walk-in customers, paying for a mainland retail unit is waste. Match the legal home to the real shape of the business — its people and its space — not to a brochure.

Answer those three, and the jurisdiction usually selects itself. Only then does price enter the conversation, and even then as a tie-breaker between suitable options, never as the thing that overrides suitability.

## The errors that cost the most

Three mistakes recur, and each is expensive enough to be worth naming plainly.

**Chasing the cheapest zone blindly.** The lowest licence price on a comparison site is a number with no context. It tells you nothing about whether that zone can license your activity, whether its visa quota fits your team, or whether its location walls you off from your market. A cheap licence that cannot reach your customers is the most expensive thing you can buy, because you pay for it and then pay again to work around it.

**Picking a zone whose activity list does not fit.** Founders fall in love with a zone's price or its address and force their business into an activity list that nearly fits. "Nearly" is the problem. The wrong activity classification limits what you can invoice for, complicates banking, and blocks the obvious expansion. Choose the jurisdiction whose list fits the business you are actually building, including its next two moves.

**Assuming a free zone licence allows a mainland shopfront.** This is the costliest error of all, and it is worth ending on. A free zone licence does not let you open a shop on a mainland street or sell directly to the mainland public. Founders sign free zone packages, then sign a mainland retail lease, and only at the licensing counter discover the two do not connect. Now they are paying for a free zone licence they cannot use as intended, a lease they cannot legally trade from, and a mainland branch or agent to bridge the gap. The wall was avoidable. It was chosen, unknowingly, on day one.

Get this decision right and the rest of setup is mechanical. Get it wrong and every later step inherits the error. With your jurisdiction settled, the next task is to turn that decision into a licence, a company, and a visa in your passport — the practical sequence, step by step. That is Chapter 3.



PART ONE — FOUNDATIONS

# Chapter 3 · Licensing, visas, and the setup sequence

*The practical mechanics — from choosing an activity to holding your Emirates ID — walked in the order you will actually walk them.*

---

## A sequence, not a pile of paperwork

With your jurisdiction chosen in Chapter 2, setting up a company in Dubai becomes what Chapter 1 promised it was: the most certain part of the journey. It is a transaction. You pay, you file, you receive a licence. The difficulty founders report is rarely that any single step is hard — it is that they do not know the steps come in an order, and that some steps cannot begin until an earlier one is finished.

So this chapter lays out the sequence as a sequence. The exact wording, the names of forms, and the screens differ between the DET on the mainland and one free zone authority and the next, and they are refreshed periodically — so treat the specifics as something to confirm against the authority you have chosen and the directory in Part Three. But the logic of the order is stable, and once you can see the order, the process stops feeling like a pile of paperwork and starts feeling like a checklist you can simply work down.

## Step one — selecting the business activity

Everything begins with the activity. Your licence does not authorise "a business" in the abstract; it authorises specific, named commercial activities, drawn from the authority's published list. A restaurant, a management consultancy, an interior fit-out contractor, a general trading company — each is a defined activity, sometimes a defined group of activities, with its own code.

Get this right first, because it propagates. The activity determines your licence type. It determines which external approvals you will need. It can determine which free zones can even license you. And it sets the boundary of what you may lawfully do once you are trading: invoicing for work outside your licensed activities is not a paperwork detail, it is operating outside your licence. Two practical instincts serve you here. Select activities that genuinely describe what you will do, including the things you will plausibly add in year two — many authorities allow a small group of related activities under one licence. But do not collect activities you will never use in the belief that more is safer; an unfocused licence can confuse a bank, and some activity combinations are not permitted together.

## Step two — the licence type

Activities are grouped into licence types, and you will be issued one of three.

**A commercial licence** covers trading and buying and selling — general trading, retail, e-commerce, foodstuff trading, building materials. **A professional licence** covers services delivered through intellectual or specialised effort — consultancy, marketing, IT services, design, accounting, legal services. **An industrial licence** covers manufacturing, processing, and assembly — the licence that authorises a factory or a production facility, and the one most likely to require physical industrial premises and additional approvals.

Most readers of this book will hold a commercial or a professional licence. The distinction is not cosmetic: it can affect ownership rules for certain activities, the documentation you must provide, and the approvals required. Confirm which type your activity sits under before you go further, because the next steps assume it.

## Step three — reserving the trade name

With activity and licence type settled, you reserve the company's name. The trade name is checked against names already registered and against the naming rules, which in the UAE are firmer than in many countries. Names must not be offensive or contradict public morals; abbreviations of personal names are generally not allowed — a company named after a person usually needs the full name; and references to religious, political, or governmental bodies are not permitted. If your chosen name implies a regulated activity, expect it to be checked against your licensed activities.

Reserving the name is a small, fast, paid step. Have two or three candidates ready so a rejection does not stall you, and reserve the name before you spend money on branding, signage, or a website built around it.

## Step four — initial approval

Initial approval is the authority's confirmation, in principle, that it has no objection to you forming this company, with these activities, under this name. It is not the licence and it does not let you trade, but it is the green light to proceed to the binding steps.

For straightforward activities, initial approval is quick. For activities that touch a specialist regulator — Dubai Municipality for food and health, the Dubai Land Department and RERA for real estate, the RTA for transport, the DTCM for tourism, the KHDA for education and training — this is the stage at which that external approval is sought, and it is the stage most able to add time. If your activity needs a third party's sign-off, identify it now; it is the variable most likely to lengthen your timeline.

## Step five — legal structure and the Memorandum of Association

Next you fix the company's legal form and ownership. The common vehicle for a small or medium business is a limited liability company, with the shareholders and their shareholdings defined in a Memorandum of Association — the MOA — which, where required, is notarised. The MOA records who owns the company, in what proportions, who manages it, and how decisions are made. Free zones typically have their own equivalent constitutional documents and their own templates.

Even if you are a sole founder owning 100 per cent of the company, do not treat this as a formality to rush. The structure you set now governs how partners are admitted, how profits are shared, and how the company is eventually sold or passed on. It is far cheaper to set the structure correctly at the start than to amend it later.

## Step six — licence issuance, premises, and the establishment card

Now the steps converge. You provide your tenancy — on the mainland, a commercial address with the lease registered through Ejari; in a free zone, the flexi-desk, shared workstation, or office that came with your package. You pay the licence fees. The authority issues the trade licence: the document that, at last, authorises you to trade.

Issued alongside or immediately after the licence is the **establishment card**, sometimes called the immigration card — the company's registration with the immigration system. This is the document that gives the company the standing to sponsor residence visas. Until it exists, you cannot apply for a visa for yourself or anyone else. Hold this point: the licence makes the company real; the establishment card makes the company an employer.

## The visa quota — and the visas themselves

Your company does not get unlimited visas. It gets a **quota** — a cap on how many residence visas it may sponsor. On the mainland the quota is linked principally to the size of your registered office space, which is one practical reason premises and headcount must be planned together. In a free zone the quota is set by your package and desk or office allocation. Plan the quota against a realistic two-year headcount, as Chapter 2 advised.

Within that quota, the founder's own visa comes in one of two forms. An **investor or partner visa** is sponsored by the company on the basis of your shareholding in it — it is the visa for an owner. An **employment visa** is the standard residence visa for staff, and a founder who also draws a salary as a manager may instead hold one of those. The practical difference is mostly in documentation and process; an adviser will tell you which suits your structure. Either way, the residence visa is what allows you to live in the UAE, open personal banking, sponsor your family, and sign a tenancy.

The visa process itself runs in a recognisable order. First an **entry permit** is issued — the document that allows the person to enter, or to remain, while the residence visa is processed. Then comes **status change** for someone already in the country, or entry on the permit for someone abroad, followed by the **medical fitness test** and the application for the **Emirates ID**. Finally the residence visa is **stamped** — historically into the passport, now increasingly issued and held in digital form — and biometrics are completed for the Emirates ID. The Emirates ID is the card you will use for almost every official interaction afterwards, so the sequence genuinely ends there.

**Staff visas** follow the same path, with the company as sponsor, drawn down against the same quota. On the mainland, employment also brings the labour-side obligations under MOHRE — the employment contract, the wage protection system, and end-of-service entitlements — which Chapter 5 will treat as part of operating responsibly.

## A realistic timeline

How long does all of this take? Honestly: from a few days to a few weeks.

A clean, common activity — a consultancy, a simple trading or e-commerce company — with no external regulator involved, full documents ready, and no premises complications, can move from initial approval to issued licence remarkably quickly, sometimes inside a week. The visa cycle for the founder then typically adds one to a few weeks on top, governed by medical and Emirates ID appointments. So a simple setup, licence through to Emirates ID, is realistically a matter of a few weeks rather than months.

What lengthens the timeline is predictable: an activity that needs a specialist regulator's approval; documents from abroad that must be attested and, if not in Arabic or English, translated; premises that need fit-out or inspection before they can be registered; or banking, which sits outside the licensing timeline entirely and runs to its own, slower clock — the subject of Chapter 4. Build your plan around the realistic case, not the fastest one a setup agent quotes you, and keep your documents and attestations ready in advance, because waiting on paperwork is the single most common cause of delay.

## A longer-term route — the Golden Visa

One residency route belongs in this chapter as a signpost rather than a step. The **Golden Visa** is a long-term renewable residence visa — issued for an extended period rather than the standard shorter cycle — available to categories such as investors, entrepreneurs, specialised professionals, and others meeting defined criteria. It is not part of the routine company-setup sequence, and most founders will start on a standard investor or employment visa. But as your business establishes itself, the Golden Visa can become a route to longer-term stability for you and your family, decoupling your residency from the annual rhythm of renewals. Note it now; revisit it once you are trading. The current criteria and thresholds move, so confirm them when the time comes.

With the licence issued, the establishment card in hand, and your Emirates ID processed, your company legally exists and you may legally trade. What it does not yet have is the thing that decides whether it survives — money, managed properly: enough capital, a working bank account, the right tax registrations, and a cash cycle you control rather than one that controls you. That is Chapter 4.



PART ONE — FOUNDATIONS

# Chapter 4 · The money — capital, banking, and cash flow

*What you truly need, why the bank account is slower than the licence, and the cash cycle that decides whether a profitable business survives.*

---

## The licence is the smallest line

Chapter 1 made a claim that this chapter now has to honour with numbers: that the licence is the smallest line in your launch budget, and that a founder with a twelve-month business and three months of money already has a cash-flow problem on day one. Your company now legally exists — Chapter 3 saw to that. Whether it survives is a question of money, and money in a Dubai business has three parts that founders tend to underestimate in the same order every time: how much capital they truly need, how hard and slow the bank account is, and how the cash cycle can drain a business that is, on paper, profitable.

We will take them in that order, and we will keep the figures concrete. As always, treat every specific number as subject to change — verify the current thresholds and confirm the costs for your industry against the Part Two chapters and the ready-reckoner in Part Three.

## How much capital you truly need

Start by separating two numbers that founders routinely merge: the cost of *opening* and the cost of *operating until the business pays for itself*. The second is far larger, and it is the one that is left out.

The cost of opening is the visible list. A basic free zone licence advertised from around AED 12,500 is, as we have said, the smallest line. Around it sit the establishment card, residence visas for you and any staff, medical tests and Emirates ID, premises or a flexi-desk, a security deposit on those premises, insurance, fit-out or equipment if your business needs them, and the early spend on branding, a website, and signage. Even for a lean, service-based business with no physical footprint, these items together typically run into the tens of thousands of dirhams. For a business with a real premises — a restaurant, a clinic, a salon, a workshop — fit-out and equipment alone can dwarf every other line, and the Part Two chapters give realistic ranges industry by industry.

The cost of operating is the number that actually decides survival. It is your rent, salaries, your own living costs, marketing, utilities, and licence and visa renewals, multiplied by the months before the business reliably covers them itself. Chapter 1 set the planning figure: most businesses in this book take six to twelve months to reach a steady footing, and a runway covering both the business and your living costs across that period is the plan, not pessimism. So the honest capital question is not "what does the licence cost" but "what is my monthly burn, and do I hold enough to fund it — with margin — for the time the business will realistically take". Answer that, and you have a capital figure you can trust. Skip it, and you have a licence and a countdown.

## The corporate bank account — the slow, compliance-heavy reality

Here is the part of the setup that most surprises founders, and the reason this chapter sits where it does: opening a corporate bank account in the UAE is slower, more demanding, and less certain than obtaining the licence itself.

Be ready for the reality rather than annoyed by it. UAE banks operate under serious compliance and anti-money-laundering obligations, and they apply them. Opening an account is not a counter transaction; it is an underwriting decision. The bank will want your trade licence, your establishment card, your shareholders' and managers' passports and visas, and the company's constitutional documents — and beyond those, it will want to understand the business. Expect questions, and expect to evidence your answers: what exactly the company does, who its customers and suppliers are, where its money will come from and go to, what your background is, and — increasingly — proof of *substance*, meaning evidence that the company is a real operating business with a real presence rather than a name on a licence. A genuine office, local customers, and a coherent activity all help. A flexi-desk with no staff, no local footprint, and an activity the bank struggles to understand will draw more scrutiny.

Two expectations will save you stress. First, time: account opening commonly takes several weeks, sometimes longer, and it runs on its own clock, entirely separate from the licensing timeline in Chapter 3. Do not plan to invoice or receive funds the week you get your licence. Second, outcome: an application can be declined, and a decline is not necessarily a judgement on you — it may simply be that your profile does not fit that bank's current appetite. If it happens, you apply elsewhere with a cleaner, fuller pack.

Choosing a bank, then, is a practical exercise, not a branding one. Weigh the things that will matter every week: minimum balance requirements and the penalties for breaching them, account and transaction fees, the quality of online and mobile banking, how the bank handles international transfers if your business needs them, and — underrated — whether the bank has genuine experience with companies of your jurisdiction and size. A bank that routinely handles free zone companies like yours will move faster than one that does not. Prepare a clear, honest business profile, expect the questions, and treat the account as something to start early and pursue patiently.

## Tax registration — VAT and corporate tax

Chapter 1 dismantled the "tax-free" myth; this chapter turns the facts into obligations you must register for.

**Value-added tax** is charged at 5 per cent and has applied since 2018. Registration with the Federal Tax Authority becomes **mandatory** once your taxable supplies pass **AED 375,000** in a twelve-month period, and it is available **voluntarily** from **AED 187,500**. Once registered, you charge VAT on your taxable sales, you may reclaim VAT on qualifying business costs, and you file periodic returns on time — late filing and late payment carry penalties. Voluntary registration before you reach the threshold can be worth considering, particularly if your customers are themselves VAT-registered businesses and your input costs carry VAT you would rather reclaim; weigh it with an adviser.

**Corporate tax** is a federal tax on business profits, in force for financial years starting on or after 1 June 2023. The rate is **0 per cent on taxable profits up to AED 375,000** and **9 per cent on profits above that threshold**. A *qualifying free zone person* may, under specific conditions, retain a 0 per cent rate on *qualifying income* — but, exactly as Chapter 2 warned, that status is conditional, it is not automatic, and it is not a headline you can simply assume. Most businesses must register for corporate tax regardless of whether they expect to owe anything, and registration and filing obligations apply even at a 0 per cent outcome. Confirm your registration deadlines and your position with an adviser; the rules are still settling, and the specifics move.

The practical point under both taxes is the same: tax in Dubai is light by world standards, but it is now an administrative discipline you must keep, not a topic you may ignore.

## Bookkeeping discipline

That discipline rests on bookkeeping, and bookkeeping is no longer optional in the UAE. VAT returns, corporate tax filings, and most free zones' audit requirements all assume you keep proper, contemporaneous accounting records, and the law expects records to be retained for a defined number of years.

Treat clean books as infrastructure, not admin. From your first transaction, keep business money and personal money in separate accounts; record income and expenses as they happen rather than reconstructing them in a panic before a deadline; keep your invoices and receipts; and either use proper accounting software or engage a bookkeeper early. A small monthly cost here prevents three expensive outcomes: penalties for late or wrong filings, a painful audit, and — the quiet one — running the business blind, without knowing month to month whether you are actually making money. Which brings us to the distinction this whole chapter turns on.

## The cash cycle — profit is an opinion, cash is a fact

Chapter 1 stated the principle; here is the mechanism. **Profit is an opinion; cash is a fact.** Profit is what your accounts say you earned over a period. Cash is what is actually in the bank on a given morning. A business can show a profit and still fail, because it runs out of cash in the gap between the two — and Dubai has a particular, well-known version of that gap.

It is the trap Chapter 1 named, and it deserves to be spelt out. On one side, **rent is frequently paid annually in advance**, in one or two cheques — a large sum leaving your account at the start of the year, before the business has earned it back. On the other side, if you sell to other businesses, **your receivables can run 60 to 90 days** — you deliver the work, you invoice, and you wait two or three months to be paid. A profitable business can be crushed in the space between a year of rent paid up front and three months of revenue not yet collected. This has ended more Dubai businesses than bad ideas ever have.

You manage it deliberately. Hold a cash buffer sized to that gap, not to your average month. Negotiate rent terms — more cheques, even at a small premium, can be worth it for the cash relief. Invoice the day work is delivered, not the week after; agree payment terms in writing before you start; ask for deposits and staged payments; and chase overdue invoices as a routine, unembarrassed discipline rather than an awkward favour. Watch your cash position weekly. Every Part Two chapter analyses the specific cash cycle of its industry for exactly this reason — because the gap is not a rare misfortune, it is the normal shape of the work, and it must be planned for rather than survived.

## Funding the business — and paying yourself

Where does the capital come from? For most readers of this book, the honest and the healthiest answer is **bootstrapping** — funding the business from your own savings and then from its own revenue. It keeps you in full control, it forces the discipline that the rest of this chapter demands, and it does not put your launch at the mercy of a third party's timeline.

The alternatives each carry a cost. **Partners** can bring capital, skills, and shared risk — but a partner is a long-term relationship, and the ownership and decision-making terms must be written down clearly at the start, in the Memorandum of Association of Chapter 3, not assumed from goodwill. **Outside investors** are realistically relevant to a narrow band of high-growth businesses, chiefly in technology; for most industries in this book, investor funding is neither available nor appropriate, and pursuing it is a distraction. As for **bank lending to a new SME**, set expectations honestly: it is limited. Banks generally want a trading history, security, and a track record before they will lend to a small company, so a brand-new business should not build its plan on a loan it does not yet have. Plan to fund the launch yourself, and treat any external funding as a possibility for later, once there is a trading record to show.

Finally, the question founders are quietly anxious about: **when do you pay yourself?** The principled answer is that your own living costs should already be inside the runway you raised — Chapter 1 was deliberate about that — so that the business is never forced to pay you a wage before it can afford one. In the early months, keep your own draw modest and predictable, pay it as a defined, recorded amount rather than dipping irregularly into the company account, and let it rise only as the cash position genuinely supports it. Paying yourself too much, too soon, is simply another way of running out of cash. Paying yourself nothing and living on fumes is not heroic either — it is the burnout that Chapter 5 will warn against. The discipline is the same as everywhere else in this chapter: plan the number, record it, and respect it.

You now have the company, the bank account, the tax registrations, and a clear-eyed view of the cash cycle. What remains is the part no licence and no spreadsheet can supply — how you actually operate, day after day, year after year. That is the difference between founders who last and founders who fold, and it is Chapter 5.



PART ONE — FOUNDATIONS

# Chapter 5 · The operating mindset — the Dubai Syndicate Way

*What separates the operators who last from the ones who fold — and how to read the field guide that follows.*

---

## The chapter that the licence cannot give you

The four chapters before this one were, in a sense, the easy part. They could be reduced to decisions, documents, and numbers — a jurisdiction chosen, a licence sequence walked, a bank account opened, a cash buffer sized. This chapter cannot be reduced that way, and that is precisely why it is the last of the foundations. The difference between a Dubai business that lasts and one that folds is rarely the licence, the zone, or the opening balance. It is the mindset of the person operating it, sustained over years.

I have watched two founders open near-identical businesses in the same month, with similar capital and similar ideas. One is still trading, steadily, several years later. The other closed inside eighteen months. The licence did not decide that. The operating mindset did. This chapter is my attempt to set that mindset down plainly — what we call, across both of these books, the Dubai Syndicate Way.

## Treat the business as a system, not a hustle

Begin with the single shift that underlies everything else. A hustle is a series of bursts: a good month, a frantic chase, a quiet stretch, another scramble. A system is a set of repeatable processes that produce a result whether or not the founder is inspired that week. The operators who last in Dubai run systems.

In practice this means the unglamorous work is built into a routine rather than left to memory and mood. How a customer is followed up, how an invoice is raised and chased, how stock is reordered, how the books are updated, how leads are recorded — each becomes a defined process that runs the same way every time. A system can be measured, handed to staff, and improved. A hustle lives only in the founder's head, breaks the moment the founder is ill or distracted, and cannot be grown or sold. Dubai is a fast and crowded market, exactly as Chapter 1 said; in a fast, crowded market the disciplined system beats the brilliant burst, because it shows up every day.

## The discipline of renewals and compliance

Nothing exposes the difference between a system and a hustle faster than renewals. A Dubai business runs on a calendar of recurring obligations, and missing one is not a minor oversight — it carries fines, it can suspend your ability to trade, and it can put visas and contracts at risk.

The recurring list is knowable, which means it is manageable. Your **trade licence** renews annually. **Residence visas** — yours and your staff's — renew on their own cycle and carry medical and Emirates ID steps. Your **Ejari** tenancy registration must be kept current, because it underpins both the licence and the visa quota. **Insurance** — and depending on your activity that may include premises, liability, and the employee health cover that is required — must be renewed before it lapses. Add VAT and corporate tax filing dates from Chapter 4, and any activity-specific permits from your regulator.

The operating discipline is simple and absolute: every one of these dates is diarised, with a reminder well before the deadline, and none is ever missed. Keep a single renewals calendar for the company, owned by a named person, reviewed monthly. This is not bureaucracy for its own sake — it is the difference between a business that compounds quietly and one that lurches from one avoidable crisis to the next. Operators who last simply do not get caught by a date they always knew was coming.

## The Dubai Syndicate Way — relationships, reputation, reciprocity, the long game

Now to the heart of it, and to the phrase on the cover of both books. The Dubai Syndicate Way is not a trick and it is not a network you can buy your way into. It is a way of operating, and it rests on four words.

**Relationships.** Dubai, for all its scale, runs on people who know people. Your bank manager, your landlord, your suppliers, your regulator's officers, the other founders in your building or your sector — these are not transactions to be completed and forgotten. They are relationships to be built before you need them. The founder who has spent two years being straightforward and reliable with a supplier is the founder who gets the call when stock is short, the extra few weeks when cash is tight, the introduction that opens a door. Build the relationship long before the day you need the favour.

**Reputation.** A city of arrivals is, paradoxically, a small town for business. Word travels. Pay your suppliers on time, deliver what you promised, and treat staff and customers decently, and that reputation precedes you into every negotiation. Cut corners, pay late, or treat people as disposable, and that reputation precedes you too — and it closes doors you will never even see close. Your reputation is the most valuable asset on a balance sheet that will never show it.

**Reciprocity.** The genuine network is not a list of contacts you extract value from. It is a web of people you have helped, who therefore help you. Make the introduction, share what you know, refer the customer who is a better fit for someone else, show up for other operators. Reciprocity given freely and consistently comes back — not always quickly, and not always from the same direction, but reliably.

**The long game.** Underneath the other three is patience. The Dubai Syndicate Way is the deliberate choice to optimise for the relationship that pays out in year three rather than the gain that is grabbed this week at someone's expense. Operators who last think in years. They would rather earn slightly less this quarter and still be trusted next year. That orientation — toward the long game — is the quiet engine behind every durable Dubai business I know.

## Hiring and managing a transient workforce

Chapter 1 made the point that Dubai is a city of arrivals and therefore also of departures, and nowhere does that bite harder than in your team. Your staff, like your customers, are mobile. People move emirates, change sectors, or leave the country. A degree of turnover is normal here and must be planned for, not resented.

So hire and manage with that reality built in. Sponsor staff properly, give them clear written contracts, meet your MOHRE and wage-protection obligations on the mainland, and provide the cover and the end-of-service entitlements the law requires — not merely because compliance is mandatory, but because reputation, as we have just said, travels through every employee who passes through your company. Beyond compliance, reduce the cost of turnover by writing down how the work is done — the systems again — so that knowledge lives in the business and not only in one person who may leave. Treat good people well enough that they stay longer than the market average, and accept that some will still go. A business that depends entirely on one irreplaceable employee is exposed in a transient city. A business that has captured its know-how in its systems is not.

## Respect the regulators

A word on posture toward authority, because it matters more here than founders from some other markets expect. Dubai's regulators — the DET, the free zone authorities, GDRFA, MOHRE, the FTA, Dubai Municipality, and the rest — are not adversaries to be outmanoeuvred. They are the infrastructure that makes the city the orderly, low-friction marketplace that drew you in the first place.

Treat them accordingly. File on time, answer questions straight, keep your paperwork in order, and when a rule changes, adapt rather than argue. The founders who thrive here treat compliance as a normal operating cost and a competitive asset — a clean record means faster approvals, easier banking, and no nasty surprises. The founders who treat regulation as an obstacle to be dodged spend their energy on a fight they will not win, and they damage the reputation that, by now, you understand is everything.

## Patience, runway, and a business that fits you

Two final pieces of mindset, both carried forward from Chapter 1.

The first is patience backed by runway. Chapter 1 set the planning figure — six to twelve months to a steady footing — and Chapter 4 turned it into a capital number. The mindset point is what those months *feel* like: slower than you hoped, quieter than the videos promised, full of unglamorous repetition. Patience is not passive waiting. It is continuing to run the system, build the relationships, and serve customers well through the stretch before the results compound — funded by a runway long enough that you are never forced into a panicked decision. A founder with runway can make good decisions. A founder out of money makes only desperate ones.

The second is fit. This entire book exists, as Chapter 1 said, to help you answer one question: does this business, with the capital, time, and temperament you actually have, fit this city. Temperament is the word to dwell on. A restaurant is a different life from a consultancy; a real estate brokerage demands a different nature from a software company. Some businesses need a person who can stand at a counter and charm strangers for twelve hours; others need someone content with quiet, technical, solitary work. Be honest with yourself about which person you are. The most reliable predictor of an operator who lasts is not the brilliance of the idea — it is the match between the daily reality of the business and the temperament of the person running it.

## How to read Part Two

That match is exactly what the rest of this book is built to help you test. You have finished the foundations. Every chapter that follows assumes them — the jurisdiction decision of Chapter 2, the licensing sequence of Chapter 3, the money discipline of Chapter 4, and the operating mindset of this chapter. From here, the book becomes a field guide.

Part Two examines thirty-five industries across eight categories — Food and Beverage, Personal and Lifestyle Services, Home and Property Services, Mobility and Transport, Trade and Commerce, Tourism and Events, Professional Services, and Tech and Specialised. You do not need to read it cover to cover. Read the categories that interest you, and use the rest as reference.

Every one of the thirty-five chapters is built on the identical eight-section template, so that you can hold any two industries side by side and compare them honestly. Each chapter tells you, in order: **what the business actually is in Dubai** — the real local version, not the generic one; the **capital required**, in realistic AED ranges for a lean start and a comfortable one; the **licence and jurisdiction** that fit it; where the **first three customers** genuinely come from, by channel and method; the **profit margins and cash cycle**, including the specific cash trap of that business; the **top three mistakes** founders make in it; **who is already winning** the niche and why; and a decisive **verdict** on who the business suits and who should avoid it.

Read each industry chapter against yourself — your capital, your time, your temperament. Use it to confirm a choice, or, just as valuably, to rule one out before it costs you anything. That is what a field guide is for. The foundations are laid. Turn the page, and let us walk the field.



# Part Two — The Field Guide

*Thirty-five industries, eight categories, one template — six pages each, built to be compared.*

---



PART TWO — THE FIELD GUIDE

# Category One · Food & Beverage

*The most visible sector in Dubai — and, per square metre, among the most demanding.*

---

Food and beverage is where Dubai's ambition shows most clearly and where its commercial gravity presses hardest. The city has more restaurant seats, more café brands, more delivery options, and more new openings per month than almost any city of comparable population. It also has some of the highest commercial rents in the world, razor-thin operating margins, and an aggregator ecosystem — Talabat, Deliveroo, Noon Food, Careem — that can take 25 to 35 per cent of every delivery order before you have paid a single bill of your own.

What binds the five chapters in this category is not cuisine or concept; it is a shared set of structural pressures. First, permitting is layered and non-negotiable: a DET trade licence, a Dubai Municipality food permit, Dubai Civil Defence approval for the fit-out, food handler cards, and occupational health certificates for every person who touches food. These are not optional extras you file for when you feel ready — they are prerequisites to operating, and they take time. Second, rent is the dominant fixed cost, paid largely in advance. A mall or hotel location gives you footfall and prestige; it also gives you a landlord who knows exactly what your footfall is worth. Street-level and community locations cost less and ask more of you in marketing. Third, the competitive field is relentless. Established brands, celebrity-chef concepts, and well-funded regional franchises all compete for the same customers and the same corner units.

Against those headwinds sit real opportunities: a youthful, spending, internationally curious population; a visitor market of close to eighteen million a year; a gifting and events culture that drives consistent demand; and a delivery infrastructure that lets a lean kitchen reach the whole city.

Read each chapter with both sides in mind. The numbers will tell you quickly whether a format fits your capital, your appetite for operational complexity, and your patience for a long payback.



PART TWO — THE FIELD GUIDE  ·  FOOD & BEVERAGE

# Chapter 6 · Restaurant & Casual Dining

*The most aspirational entry point in Dubai's food scene — and the one with the most unforgiving economics.*

---

## 1 · What this business actually is in Dubai

A restaurant in Dubai is not a single thing. It ranges from a 30-seat neighbourhood eatery in a residential community to a 200-cover mall anchor with a celebrity consultant and a multi-year fit-out budget. What they share is the same permitting stack, the same labour-intensive service model, and the same brutal fixed-cost structure.

The typical casual-dining restaurant in Dubai operates in one of three settings. A mall unit delivers built-in footfall but commands some of the highest rents in the region, charged annually and often in advance. A street-level or strip-mall location in a residential community — JBR, Al Barsha, Jumeirah, Mirdif — costs significantly less but requires you to generate your own pull. A hotel or serviced-apartment building can offer both a captive breakfast crowd and walk-in evening trade, at terms that vary widely by negotiation.

The customer base is genuinely diverse. Dubai's restaurant market spans every income level and every cuisine. Expat nostalgia, tourist curiosity, and the local Emirati preference for generous hospitality each create distinct demand profiles. Where founders often go wrong is building a concept for the Dubai they imagined — aspirational, premium, always full — rather than the Dubai they will actually encounter: price-sensitive on weekdays, heavily occasion-driven, and willing to travel only if the reason is convincing.

Deliveries are not optional. Even a sit-down restaurant must operate on Talabat, Deliveroo, or both to capture the segment of the market that orders in. That income comes with a commission attached, which compresses already thin margins further. Expect the business to run two revenue streams simultaneously from day one.

---

## 2 · Capital required

A casual restaurant is a capital-heavy business before it serves a single meal. The figures below are indicative at the time of writing — confirm current costs with contractors and the relevant authorities, and consult the ready-reckoner in Part Three.

**Trade licence (DET, mainland):** AED 12,000–18,000 for the first year, depending on activity scope and whether a food service activity is added to a general trading licence or held separately.

**Dubai Municipality food permit:** AED 2,000–5,000 at the time of writing, renewable annually; exact fees vary by establishment category.

**Fit-out:** This is the dominant variable. A lean 40-seat fit-out in a community location can be completed for AED 150,000–250,000 if you are disciplined with materials and contractors. A mall unit with landlord-specified finishes, a full commercial kitchen, and branded décor typically runs AED 400,000–900,000 or more. Dubai Civil Defence approval for the fit-out is mandatory and must be budgeted into both cost and time.

**Equipment:** Commercial kitchen equipment — ranges, ovens, refrigeration, extraction — adds AED 60,000–150,000 depending on cuisine and output volume.

**Staff and visas:** A modest team of eight to twelve people covering kitchen, floor, and management carries a monthly payroll of AED 35,000–65,000, plus visa costs of AED 4,000–7,000 per person.

**Deposits and miscellaneous:** Landlord deposits of one to three months' rent, utility connections, DEWA deposits, and first-year insurance.

**Working capital:** Six months of operating costs — the true test of readiness.

A lean community restaurant requires a realistic first-year budget of AED 500,000–750,000. A mid-scale mall concept should be planned at AED 1,000,000–1,800,000. These are not generous estimates; they are honest ones.

---

## 3 · Licence & jurisdiction

A restaurant on the mainland requires a DET trade licence with a restaurant or food service activity. You cannot operate a food business without a simultaneous Dubai Municipality food establishment permit — these are two separate approvals from two separate authorities, and neither substitutes for the other.

The DET licence is the entity's legal permission to trade. The Municipality permit is the location-specific, food-specific approval that authorises you to prepare and serve food at that address. Any change of premises means a new permit application.

The fit-out itself — exhaust systems, fire suppression, emergency exits, signage — requires Dubai Civil Defence sign-off before you can open. Factor three to eight weeks for this process alongside construction.

Every person who handles food must hold a valid occupational health card issued through the DHA (Dubai Health Authority), and at least one person per shift must hold a Person-in-Charge (PIC) food safety certificate, which involves a structured training programme and examination.

A free zone licence is not appropriate for a physical restaurant serving the public. Restaurants trade in a specific location on the mainland; the mainland is where your licence must sit. Some free zone jurisdictions exist within hospitality developments (DIFC, for instance, has its own food permit regime), but for a community or mall restaurant, DET is the correct authority.

The practical recommendation is to engage a licensing consultant who has handled food-service businesses specifically. The activity wording on your DET licence matters: an incorrect activity list can block your Municipality permit or limit your menu scope.

---

## 4 · First three customers

A new restaurant cannot wait for word-of-mouth to build organically. The first three customers — real, paying, and returning — come from deliberate early action.

**Soft-launch guests.** A controlled soft launch, invitation-only for two to four weeks, serves two purposes: it stress-tests the kitchen and the service, and it puts 40 to 80 people in the room who chose to come and who have something at stake in your success. These are friends, contacts, community leaders, and local food bloggers. Ask them for honest feedback; ask them to return and bring one person they know.

**Delivery platform visibility.** List on Talabat and Deliveroo from week one. Both platforms offer promotional packages for new listings — reduced commission, featuring on discovery pages — that are worth using for the first 90 days. Your first dozen delivery orders may come from people who have never heard of you but found you browsing the app on a Tuesday evening. Do not dismiss those customers; they are your early review base.

**Community and building management relationships.** If you are in a residential community, introduce yourself to the building management and the community manager. Offer a staff lunch; offer a neighbour discount for the first month. The objective is to be *known* before residents have had any reason to seek you out. The restaurant the community manager recommends to new arrivals gets a disproportionate share of the first-week spend from every new resident.

None of this is glamorous. All of it works.

---

## 5 · Profit margins & cash cycle

Gross margins in a casual restaurant typically run between 60 and 70 per cent of food revenue — that is, food costs take 30 to 40 per cent of turnover. These figures sound healthy until you stack the fixed costs beneath them.

Rent in a community location might cost AED 15,000–35,000 per month. A mid-range mall unit could cost AED 40,000–90,000 or more. Staff is the second large fixed cost. Together, rent and payroll routinely consume 50 to 65 per cent of total revenue in the early years, leaving a net margin — if the restaurant is performing — of somewhere between 8 and 15 per cent of revenue. Many restaurants operate below that for the first 18 months, and some never climb above it.

The cash trap is structural. Rent is paid in advance — often two cheques covering six months each. Your food supplier requires payment in 30 days. Your delivery platforms settle on a weekly or fortnightly cycle. Your dine-in customers pay on the night. The net result is a business where money goes out in large lumps and comes back in small, frequent increments. You must ensure the cash on hand at the beginning of every month is enough to cover the next two large outgoings, not just the expected daily revenue.

Breakeven at a community restaurant — real, month-on-month cash breakeven — typically takes nine to 18 months. At a mall location with higher rent and higher footfall potential, the curve is steeper in both directions. Budget for 12 to 24 months.

---

## 6 · Top 3 mistakes

**Overspending the fit-out.** The single most common route to an early failure is a founder who spends AED 800,000 on a beautiful interior, opens with AED 50,000 in working capital, and then discovers that even a full restaurant three nights a week cannot cover rent, payroll, and food cost from a standing start. The fit-out is not the business; it is the housing for the business. Spend what the concept genuinely requires, not what looks right in a render. Every dirham diverted to marble that should have been working capital extends the time to breakeven — and in a restaurant, time is the scarcest resource.

**Underestimating the permitting timeline.** Founders consistently expect the gap between signing a lease and opening to be six to eight weeks. The realistic figure, once Municipality permits, Civil Defence approvals, staff health cards, and DET amendments are in place, is four to six months — sometimes longer. Rent continues through every week of that period. Build the permitting timeline into your financial model before you sign anything, and add a buffer of four to six weeks on top of whatever you are told.

**Ignoring aggregator margins.** The delivery platforms generate revenue, and new operators are rightly grateful for them. What the revenue obscures is the cost structure beneath it. At a commission of 25 to 35 per cent of order value (confirm current rates with each platform, as these move), a AED 60 order yields AED 39–45 to the restaurant before any packaging or rider tip considerations. On a dish that cost AED 20 to prepare, the margin on that delivery sale may be lower than on an equivalent dine-in order, even before accounting for packaging. Price your delivery menu with this arithmetic in front of you, not as an afterthought.

---

## 7 · Who's already winning

The operators holding ground in Dubai's casual restaurant market share a recognisable set of characteristics. They chose their location with discipline — street-level or community locations with negotiated rent caps, or mall units in developments where management actively drives footfall. They are not the largest restaurant in the mall; they are the best-valued one on a Tuesday.

They have a focused menu. Not six pages of options — two pages with real depth. A focused kitchen runs faster, wastes less, and trains staff more effectively. Every time a restaurant adds a dish "because a customer asked," the kitchen slows down.

They operate a hybrid revenue model from the start: dine-in anchors the brand, delivery generates volume, and at least one corporate or recurring revenue strand — a weekly office lunch contract, a standing catering order, a school or gym nutrition partnership — provides baseline cash flow that is not dependent on walk-ins.

They also have someone in charge of the numbers daily. Not monthly. The restaurant owner who checks the cash position weekly is always surprised; the one who checks it every morning rarely is. The operational winners in this market are operators first and chefs or restaurateurs second.

---

## 8 · Verdict

A casual restaurant in Dubai is viable if you have genuine hospitality experience, a disciplined approach to money, and a start-up budget that does not end at the fit-out. It is not a passive business, and it is not a shortcut to lifestyle. The margins are real but narrow, the competition is significant, and the operational demands — daily stock, daily staff, daily customer interaction — leave little margin for distraction.

Who should proceed: an operator with hands-on experience, AED 700,000 or more in accessible capital, a specific and defensible location choice, and a clear answer to who their first 100 customers are.

Who should pause: anyone whose plan depends on a prime mall location on a lean budget, or who expects to hire a manager and step back within the first year.

**Bottom line:** Dubai has room for more good restaurants — and almost no room for undercapitalised ones.

---



PART TWO — THE FIELD GUIDE  ·  FOOD & BEVERAGE

# Chapter 7 · Café & Coffee Shop

*A business that looks simple from the outside and rewards precision on the inside.*

---

Dubai has more coffee shops per square kilometre in its commercial districts than most cities in the world. The category draws founders who love the product, enjoy the social atmosphere, and underestimate, almost universally, how little margin there is between a thriving café and one that closes after eight months.

## 1 · What this business actually is in Dubai

A café in Dubai sits on a spectrum. At one end is the small-format specialty coffee bar — 15 to 25 seats, a single origin espresso programme, a short food menu, and a loyal community following. At the other end is a full casual-dining café with a kitchen producing brunch, lunch, and all-day bites, a full coffee bar, and a retail shelf of branded merchandise. These are not the same business. They share a name and a coffee machine; their capital requirements, staffing structures, and margin profiles are quite different.

What is consistent across the category is the customer expectation. Dubai's café drinkers are internationally literate — many arrive from cities with established third-wave coffee cultures, and they compare. They also expect an environment as much as a beverage. A café in this market is selling a place to sit, to work, to be seen, and to meet people, and the coffee is the ticket of entry. That expectation raises both the design standard and the rental benchmark, because a bad environment fails the product even when the product is good.

The café market splits along two axes: location and daypart. A Jumeirah or DIFC café can charge AED 30–40 for a flat white and fill seats from morning to late afternoon. A residential-community café may charge AED 18–25 and depend heavily on the breakfast and mid-morning rush. The CBD café near an office tower fills from 7 to 10 a.m. and 1 to 2 p.m., then empties. Each of these formats has a different revenue model — different seating efficiency, different average spend per cover, different dependence on delivery. Know which format you are building before you sign a lease.

The delivery component is smaller in a café than in a restaurant, but it is not zero. Most cafés list on at least one aggregator, primarily for grab-and-go orders and lunch boxes. Commission at 25–35% compresses an already narrow per-unit margin on drinks; the arithmetic works better on food items, which is one reason the most profitable cafés carry a substantive food menu.

---

## 2 · Capital required

The figures below are indicative at the time of writing; verify current costs and consult the ready-reckoner in Part Three before committing capital.

**Trade licence (DET, mainland):** AED 12,000–18,000 for year one, as with other food businesses. The activity must specifically cover café or coffee shop operations.

**Dubai Municipality food permit:** AED 2,000–5,000 at the time of writing, location-specific and renewable annually.

**Fit-out:** A compact specialty café — 20 to 30 seats, clean but not extravagant — can be fitted for AED 120,000–220,000. A larger all-day café with a full kitchen, branded interiors, and a custom coffee bar counter typically runs AED 280,000–550,000. Dubai Civil Defence approval must be secured before opening; build three to six weeks and AED 3,000–8,000 in associated fees into the timeline.

**Equipment:** A commercial espresso machine, grinders, refrigeration, and a simple food-prep counter will run AED 35,000–80,000. A specialty machine from a premium manufacturer, bought new, can cost AED 45,000–90,000 on its own. Used equipment sourced from reputable suppliers can reduce this line meaningfully.

**Staff and visas:** A lean café team of four to seven — a barista, a floor person, a kitchen hand, and a manager — runs AED 18,000–35,000 per month in payroll. Visa costs are AED 4,000–7,000 per person.

**Working capital:** A minimum of six months of fixed costs before the café generates any revenue.

A realistic lean start for a small specialty café runs AED 350,000–550,000 all-in. A full-service all-day café should be planned at AED 650,000–950,000.

---

## 3 · Licence & jurisdiction

A café requires a DET trade licence with a specific food service or café activity, and a Dubai Municipality food establishment permit for the premises. The two are separate approvals from separate authorities. Neither substitutes for the other.
