PART THREE — RESOURCES

# Part Three · Resources

*Where the decisions of this book become an application, a budget, and a plan.*

---

Parts One and Two were about judgement — whether Dubai fits you, and whether a particular industry does. Part Three is about execution. It is the section you return to once you have decided, when the question is no longer *should I* but *how, and in what order, and what will it cost*.

You should use it as a companion, not a replacement. Part One told you why the jurisdiction decision matters; the comparison in this part lays the options side by side so you can make it. Each Part Two chapter gave you a capital range for one industry; the ready-reckoner here shows you the line items behind any range, so you can build a budget that is yours rather than borrowed. The 90-day launch plan sequences the work so nothing important is discovered late. The directory tells you which authority owns which decision. The glossary translates the vocabulary you will meet from the first day onward.

One standing caveat governs everything that follows, and it is the same caveat carried through every chapter of this book. Fees change. Thresholds change. Procedures, forms, portals, and the names of departments change — sometimes between one financial year and the next. Every figure in Part Three is indicative: a realistic range to budget against, never a quote. Every contact route is a starting point, not a guarantee. Before you act on any number or any procedure in this section, confirm the current position directly with the relevant authority through its official channel. Treat Part Three as a well-drawn map. The territory is still the authority's to define, and it is your responsibility to check the map against the ground before you commit money to it.



PART THREE — RESOURCES

# The 90-day launch plan

*From decision to first customer — the work, in the order it should be done.*

---

Ninety days is enough time to take a Dubai business from a decision to an open door, provided the work is done in the right sequence. Most of the delay founders experience is not caused by slow authorities; it is caused by steps attempted out of order — a bank account sought before a licence exists, a shop fitted out before its activity was approved, visas applied for before an establishment card was issued. This plan puts the steps in a sequence that does not double back on itself.

Treat the day numbers as a rhythm, not a deadline. A consultancy with no premises and one visa can compress this to a few weeks. A restaurant with a kitchen, a fit-out, and ten visas may need longer than 90 days, and that is normal. Use the phase structure to know what should be finished before the next phase begins.

## Phase 1 · Decide and validate (days 1–15)

Before any money goes to a licence, the business has to survive contact with reality.

**Confirm the business with Parts One and Two.** Re-read Chapter 1 honestly: do you have a real runway, demand you have tested, and the temperament for a fast, crowded market? Then read the relevant Part Two chapter twice — once for the capital and once for the "first three customers" section.

**Validate demand, not enthusiasm.** Speak to at least ten people who would actually pay you. Better still, secure a written expression of interest, a pre-order, or a letter of intent. A founder who cannot find ten interested strangers in their own network has learned something cheaply.

**Build the real budget.** Using the ready-reckoner in this part, write down every line — licence, approvals, establishment card, visas, Emirates ID, premises, fit-out, insurance, accounting, and working capital. Add a runway of six to twelve months for both the business and your own living costs.

**Choose your activity precisely.** The exact activity wording on your licence determines what you may legally do and which approvals you need. Decide it now, not at the counter.

By the end of Phase 1 you should have a validated idea, a written budget, a runway figure, and a clear activity. If any of these is missing, do not move to Phase 2.

## Phase 2 · Choose the jurisdiction and reserve the name (days 15–30)

**Make the jurisdiction decision.** Using the comparison in this part and Chapter 2, decide between mainland, a free zone, and offshore. The deciding question is where your customers are and who they are: sell to the UAE public or hold a government contract, and mainland is usually indicated; serve international or business clients from a desk, and a free zone often wins; structure or hold assets with no UAE trading, and offshore exists for that narrow purpose.

**Shortlist the specific authority.** If mainland, that is DET. If a free zone, shortlist two or three whose package, location, and activity list fit you, and compare their true first-year cost rather than the headline licence price.

**Reserve a trade name and seek initial approval.** Submit two or three name options that meet the naming rules, and obtain initial approval — the authority's confirmation, in principle, that it has no objection to your proposed activity and owners. Begin gathering documents: passports, photographs, proof of address, and any attestations or no-objection certificates your situation requires.

By the end of Phase 2 you should hold a reserved name and an initial approval, and know exactly which authority will issue your licence.

## Phase 3 · Licence, premises, and establishment card (days 30–55)

**Secure premises or a flexi-desk.** Your licence needs a registered address. A flexi-desk or shared-desk package satisfies this for many service businesses; a shop, clinic, or kitchen needs real premises and an Ejari-registered tenancy. Do not sign a long commercial lease until your activity is approved — a refused approval on a leased unit is an expensive lesson.

**Complete licensing approvals.** Sign the memorandum of association or the equivalent free zone documents, pay the fees, and clear any special approvals your activity triggers — Dubai Municipality for food and health, Dubai Civil Defence for fit-outs, DTCM for tourism, KHDA for training, and so on. These external approvals are the most common cause of delay; start them as early as the authority allows.

**Receive the trade licence.** This is the milestone, not the finish line. With the licence issued, apply for the establishment card (also called the immigration card) — the document that authorises your company to sponsor visas. Nothing on the visa side can begin without it.

By the end of Phase 3 you should hold a trade licence, a registered address, and an establishment card.

## Phase 4 · Visas, banking, and registration (days 55–75)

**Process residence visas.** With the establishment card in hand, apply for entry permits, then complete status change, the medical fitness test, biometrics, and Emirates ID for yourself and any staff. Run these in parallel where the authority permits; do not queue them one behind another.

**Open the corporate bank account.** Expect this to be the slowest and least predictable step. Banks apply their own compliance standards and may ask for a business plan, proof of activity, tenancy, and details of expected transactions and counterparties. Prepare a clear, honest file, apply to more than one bank, and budget several weeks. Do not assume an account is automatic because the licence is.

**Register for tax.** Register for corporate tax as required, and register for VAT if your taxable supplies meet the mandatory threshold or you elect to register voluntarily. The Federal Tax Authority operates these registrations; confirm the current thresholds and deadlines directly with it.

**Engage an accountant or bookkeeper from day one.** Clean books are cheaper to maintain than to reconstruct, and you will need them for tax, for renewals, and for the bank.

By the end of Phase 4 you should have visas in progress or issued, a bank account open or close to it, and your tax registrations filed.

## Phase 5 · Open and find the first customers (days 75–90)

**Complete fit-out and final inspections.** Finish premises works and pass any civil defence, municipality, or authority inspection your activity requires before you trade.

**Stand up operations.** Put in place the unglamorous infrastructure: a payment solution, insurance cover, basic systems, suppliers, stock, and your first staff onboarded properly.

**Execute the "first three customers" plan.** Return to the named, channel-specific plan from your Part Two chapter and work it deliberately. Opening day is the start of the real work, not its conclusion.

**Set the renewal calendar.** Record every renewal date — licence, establishment card, visas, Emirates ID, Ejari, insurance — the day each is issued. Missed renewals carry fines that are entirely avoidable.

At day 90 you should be trading, compliant, and executing for revenue. The launch is over; the business has begun. Confirm every fee, threshold, and procedure in this plan with the relevant authority before you act — all of them are subject to change.



PART THREE — RESOURCES

# Free zone vs mainland — the comparison

*The decision that sets your costs, your customers, and your licence in a single stroke.*

---

This is the first structural decision a Dubai founder makes, and it shapes every decision after it. Chapter 2 made the case in full; this resource lays the three options side by side so you can choose with the facts in front of you. There is no universally correct answer. There is only the answer that fits where your customers are, who they are, and what you intend to do.

## The three options, plainly

**Mainland.** A mainland company is licensed by DET — the Dubai Department of Economy and Tourism — and may trade directly anywhere in the UAE market: to the public, to other businesses, in shops and offices across the city, and with government bodies. For the large majority of commercial and industrial activities, 100% foreign ownership has been permitted since the 2021 reform; a limited set of strategic activities still carries ownership restrictions, so confirm your specific activity. The mainland is the natural home of any business whose customer is the UAE public or whose work requires an unrestricted local presence.

**Free zone.** A free zone company is licensed by one of Dubai's 40-plus free zone authorities, each with its own regime, activity list, and pricing. Full foreign ownership has always been the rule. A free zone company trades freely within its own zone and internationally, and a qualifying free zone person may, under specific conditions, retain a 0% corporate tax rate on qualifying income. The traditional limit is that selling directly into the mainland UAE market is not as straightforward as it is for a mainland company — it is typically done through a mainland distributor, a branch, or other arrangements. Free zones suit businesses serving international or business clients, and many offer fast, packaged, predictable setup.

**Offshore.** An offshore company — for example JAFZA Offshore or RAK ICC — is a holding and structuring vehicle. It is used for holding shares, owning intellectual property, owning property where permitted, and international structuring. It has no UAE operating presence and grants no residence visas. It is not a way to run a trading business in Dubai; it is a way to hold one.

## Comparison table

| Factor | Mainland | Free zone | Offshore |
|---|---|---|---|
| Licensing authority | DET | One of 40-plus free zone authorities | Offshore registry (e.g. JAFZA, RAK ICC) |
| Foreign ownership | 100% for most activities; some strategic activities restricted | 100%, always | 100% |
| Where you may trade | Anywhere in the UAE, including to the public and government | Within the zone and internationally; into the mainland via a distributor or branch | No UAE trading; holding and structuring only |
| Residence visas | Yes, subject to office space and quotas | Yes, subject to the package and facility | None |
| Premises | Real office or unit usually required; address must be registered | Flexi-desk to full unit, depending on package | Not applicable |
| Corporate tax | 9% above AED 375,000; 0% below | 9% standard; 0% on qualifying income for a qualifying free zone person | Subject to its own treatment; confirm |
| Audit | Required for some activities; confirm | Required by many zones | Often required by the registry |
| Cost shape | Variable; driven by activity, approvals, and real premises | Often a packaged, predictable headline price | Low and predictable; a structuring fee, not an operating cost |
| Banking perception | Generally well understood by banks | Well understood; some zones carry stronger reputations | Can attract more compliance scrutiny |

Every figure and rule above is indicative and subject to change. Confirm the current position with the relevant authority before you decide.

## The main types of free zone

"Free zone" is not one thing. Dubai's zones cluster into a few broad types, and matching the type to your business matters as much as the price.

**Commodity and trading zones.** Zones built around trade and commodities — DMCC is the best known — suit general trading, commodities, and businesses that value a strong, bank-friendly reputation.

**Sector and industry zones.** Zones organised around an industry — media, technology, healthcare, design, education — bring a relevant community, tailored activity lists, and sometimes sector regulators on site. A media or tech founder often benefits from being inside the right cluster.

**Logistics and industrial zones.** Zones attached to ports, airports, and industrial land — Jebel Ali and Dubai South among them — suit manufacturing, warehousing, and logistics, where proximity to freight infrastructure is the asset.

**Financial zones.** The DIFC operates under its own legal and regulatory framework and suits financial services, funds, and firms that want a common-law environment.

**General-purpose, low-cost zones.** Several zones — IFZA and Meydan are common examples — compete on price, speed, and a broad activity list, and suit lean service businesses and first-time founders who want a straightforward, predictable package.

## How to choose a free zone

If a free zone is right for you, choose the specific one on five tests, in order. **Activity:** does the zone license your exact activity? This is a veto — no further questions if the answer is no. **True cost:** compare the genuine first-year total, including visas, the establishment card, and the desk, not the advertised licence. **Premises:** does the package give you the space and visa quota you actually need? **Reputation:** some zones are better recognised by banks and clients, which eases account opening and credibility. **Location and community:** physical convenience and being among relevant peers both have real value.

## The decision guide

Strip the decision to one question: **where are your customers, and who are they?**

If your customers are the **UAE public** — a restaurant, salon, clinic, retail shop, or any business needing to serve walk-in or local customers directly — choose **mainland**. The same applies if you need to bid for government work or require an unrestricted local footprint.

If your customers are **international or business clients** served from an office or remotely — consultancy, software, media, trading with a focus on export and re-export, agencies — a **free zone** is often the better fit, for full ownership, packaged setup, and the qualifying-income tax position.

If you intend to **sell into the UAE market from a free zone**, plan from the start for a distributor, a mainland branch, or a dual-licence arrangement, and cost it in. Do not assume the mainland sale is free.

If you are **holding assets, shares, or intellectual property** with no UAE operations and no need for visas, an **offshore** company is the right and economical tool — and only for that.

Choose for the next three years, not the next three weeks. Switching jurisdictions later is possible but rarely cheap. The right first choice is the one that matches the customer you are actually building the business to serve.



PART THREE — RESOURCES

# The authority and regulator directory

*Who governs what — and when in your journey each one matters.*

---

A Dubai business does not answer to a single office. It answers to a small set of authorities, each with its own remit, and the order in which you meet them is largely fixed by the setup sequence in Chapter 3 and the launch plan in this part. This directory is a map of that set: for each authority, what it governs and the point at which a founder needs it. Use it to know in advance which door a given decision belongs behind, so that an approval is never discovered late.

One caveat governs the entire directory, and it is not a formality. The names of departments, the structure of portals, the location of service centres, and the published contact routes all change — some have changed more than once in recent years. This directory deliberately does not print phone numbers or counter addresses, because a wrong number is worse than none. For every authority below, reach it through its current official website or its official app, and confirm any procedure or fee directly before you act on it.

## The authorities you will almost certainly meet

**DET — Dubai Department of Economy and Tourism.** Formerly the DED. DET licenses every mainland company in Dubai and governs mainland trade names, initial approvals, activity lists, and licence renewals. If your jurisdiction decision points to the mainland, DET is the issuing authority for your licence and the office you renew with each year. Its Trakheesi system handles many mainland permits and approvals. You need DET from the moment you choose mainland, and every year thereafter at renewal.

**The free zone authorities.** Dubai has 40-plus free zones, and each one is its own licensing authority — DMCC, IFZA, Meydan, Dubai South, DIFC, Dubai CommerCity, Dubai Internet City, and many more. If you set up in a free zone, that zone's authority is your equivalent of DET: it issues your licence, registers your name, provides your premises or flexi-desk, and in most cases processes your establishment card and visa applications through its own one-stop channel. You need your chosen free zone authority from jurisdiction selection through to every renewal. The DIFC additionally operates its own independent legal and regulatory framework, which matters if you set up there.

**GDRFA — General Directorate of Residency and Foreigners Affairs.** GDRFA governs entry permits, residence visas, and the residency status of you and everyone you sponsor. Every founder who intends to live in the UAE, and every business that intends to employ resident staff, deals with GDRFA — directly on the mainland, or via the free zone's channel inside a zone. You need GDRFA once your establishment card is issued and the visa stage begins, and again at every visa renewal.

**The FTA — Federal Tax Authority.** The FTA administers both VAT, in force at 5% since 2018, and the federal corporate tax of 9% on profits above AED 375,000. It handles tax registration, returns, and deregistration. You need the FTA after your licence is issued: for corporate tax registration as required, and for VAT registration once your taxable supplies meet the mandatory threshold or you choose to register voluntarily. Confirm current thresholds and deadlines with the FTA directly — these have moved before and may move again.

## The authorities you meet if your activity calls for them

Not every founder deals with every authority below. Which ones apply to you is decided by your activity — which is why Chapter 3 insists you fix your activity wording early.

**MOHRE — Ministry of Human Resources and Emiratisation.** MOHRE governs labour and employment for mainland companies: employment contracts, work permits, the WPS payroll system, and Emiratisation obligations. Its Tasheel service centres process much of this work. A mainland employer needs MOHRE from the first hire onward. Free zone companies handle employment through their own zone authority rather than MOHRE; confirm which framework applies to you.

**Dubai Municipality.** The Municipality governs food safety, public health, and building and environmental permits. Any business handling food — restaurant, café, cloud kitchen, catering, bakery — needs Municipality approval for its premises and food licence. Salons, spas, and other health-adjacent businesses face health permits here too. You need the Municipality during the premises and approvals phase, before you may trade.

**Dubai Civil Defence.** Civil Defence governs fire safety and life safety: fit-out approvals, fire systems, and the inspections that clear a physical premises for occupation. Any founder fitting out a shop, kitchen, clinic, studio, or warehouse needs Civil Defence sign-off before opening, and it is among the most common causes of delay — begin it as early as the fit-out allows.

**Dubai Land Department and RERA.** The DLD regulates real estate, and RERA — the Real Estate Regulatory Agency within it — regulates the profession: brokerage, broker permits, the broker examination, and property management. A real estate brokerage or property manager needs the DLD and RERA before trading. Any founder signing a commercial tenancy also meets the DLD's Ejari system, which registers the lease.

**The RTA — Roads and Transport Authority.** The RTA governs vehicles, transport, and mobility permits — relevant to car rental, chauffeur and limousine services, delivery and last-mile logistics, and any business operating vehicles for hire. If your activity puts vehicles on the road commercially, you need RTA permits before you operate. Salik, the road-toll system, sits under the RTA's wider remit.

**DTCM — Dubai's tourism authority within DET.** DTCM governs tourism activity: tour operators, travel agencies, desert safari operators, hotels, and holiday-home rentals. It also administers the Tourism Dirham levy on guest stays. A tourism, travel, or holiday-homes founder needs DTCM approval as part of licensing.

**KHDA — Knowledge and Human Development Authority.** KHDA governs private education and training: schools, nurseries, training institutes, and tutoring centres. If your activity is education or training, KHDA approval is part of your licensing and is required before you may enrol students.

## How to use this directory

Read your chosen Part Two industry chapter alongside this page. Section 3 of each chapter — *Licence and jurisdiction* — names the specific approvals that industry triggers. Cross-reference those named approvals here to understand what each authority governs and when, in the 90-day plan, you will need it. Then, and only then, confirm the current procedure and any fee with that authority through its official channel. The map tells you which door to knock on; the authority behind it sets the rules, and those rules are theirs to change.



PART THREE — RESOURCES

# The cost ready-reckoner

*The line items behind any launch budget — and how to build one that is yours.*

---

Every Part Two chapter gave you a capital range for one industry. This resource shows you the parts that make up any such range, so that you can build a budget from the ground rather than borrow one. A range you have assembled yourself, line by line, is a budget you can defend to a bank, a partner, and — most importantly — to yourself at month eight, when the early optimism has worn off and only the numbers remain.

Read this page with one fact held firmly in mind. Every figure here is **indicative**: a realistic order of magnitude to plan against, not a quotation. Costs in Dubai vary by activity, by jurisdiction, by the specific free zone, by the size of premises, and by the year. They move. Treat each range below as a starting estimate, then confirm the current figure for your exact situation with the relevant authority, using the directory in this part. A budget built on indicative ranges and then verified is sound. A budget built on a single advertised headline is not.

## The cost lines a founder should budget for

**The licence.** The headline number, and the smallest part of the truth. A basic free zone licence is advertised from roughly AED 12,500 a year; mainland licence costs vary more widely with activity and approvals. *Indicative: AED 12,500–30,000 a year for many small businesses, with some activities materially higher.* Budget the licence as one line among many, never as the cost of opening.

**Registration and approvals.** Trade name reservation, initial approval, the memorandum of association or equivalent, and any activity-specific external approvals — Dubai Municipality, Dubai Civil Defence, DTCM, KHDA, RERA and others. *Indicative: AED 2,000–15,000 in total, depending heavily on how many external approvals your activity triggers.* A consultancy may sit at the bottom of this range; a restaurant or clinic well above it.

**The establishment card.** Also called the immigration card — the document that lets your company sponsor visas. *Indicative: AED 1,000–2,500, with periodic renewal.* Small, but non-negotiable: nothing on the visa side begins without it.

**Residence visas.** Entry permit, status change, stamping, and the visa itself, for the founder and each employee. *Indicative: AED 3,500–7,000 per person, per visa cycle.* A solo founder budgets one; a business with staff multiplies this line by every head, and it is among the most commonly underestimated costs in a launch.

**Emirates ID and medical fitness tests.** Each visa requires a medical fitness test and an Emirates ID. *Indicative: AED 700–1,500 per person combined.* Modest individually; budget it for everyone you sponsor.

**Premises and Ejari.** The registered address your licence requires. A flexi-desk or shared-desk package may satisfy a service business; a shop, clinic, kitchen, or warehouse needs real premises and an Ejari-registered tenancy. *Indicative: AED 12,000–25,000 a year for a flexi-desk or small shared office; AED 40,000–150,000-plus a year for a real commercial unit, by size and location.* Remember the Dubai rhythm from Chapter 1: rent is often paid annually in advance, in one or two cheques, plus a security deposit and agency fee. Budget the whole year's rent as a day-one outflow, not a monthly one.

**Fit-out.** The cost of making premises fit to trade — design, construction, fixtures, signage, and Civil Defence-compliant systems. *Indicative: from a few thousand dirhams for a light office refresh to AED 150,000–500,000-plus for a restaurant kitchen or a clinic.* This line varies more than any other; the figure in your Part Two chapter is the better guide for your industry.

**Insurance.** Cover appropriate to your activity — public liability, professional indemnity, property, and the medical insurance you are obliged to provide to sponsored staff. *Indicative: AED 1,500–10,000 a year for a small business, more with employees and higher-risk activities.*

**Accounting and bookkeeping.** Engaging a bookkeeper or accountant from day one, as Chapter 4 and the launch plan both insist. *Indicative: AED 6,000–24,000 a year for a small business, depending on transaction volume and whether an audit is required.* Clean books are far cheaper to maintain than to reconstruct.

**Working capital.** The line founders most often omit, and the one that decides whether the business survives its first year. This is the money that funds operations — stock, salaries, rent, marketing, utilities — before the business reliably pays for itself. It is not a setup fee; it is the fuel. Size it from your runway, not from a rule of thumb.

## Building a realistic first-year budget

Add the lines above for your specific business, using the figures in your Part Two chapter to sharpen the ranges. Then apply three disciplines.

**Separate setup costs from running costs.** Setup costs — licence, registration, establishment card, first visas, fit-out — are one-off and largely front-loaded. Running costs — rent, salaries, insurance, accounting, marketing, utilities — recur every month whether or not a customer has appeared. A founder who counts only the first group has budgeted to open a business, not to operate one.

**Add a contingency.** Approvals take longer than planned, a quote comes in higher, an inspection requires a change. A contingency of 15–20% over your assembled total is prudence, not pessimism. If it is not spent, it becomes runway.

**Plan the runway openly.** Chapter 1 set the standard: a cash runway covering both the business and your own living costs across six to twelve months is the plan, not the worst case. Most businesses in this book take that long to reach a steady footing. Calculate your monthly running cost, multiply by your honest months-to-breakeven, and treat that figure as a budget line as real as the licence.

## A worked shape

For a lean, single-founder service business in a free zone, a defensible first-year total often lands somewhere around **AED 60,000–120,000** once licence, one visa, a flexi-desk, insurance, accounting, and a modest working-capital cushion are counted. For a premises-based business — a restaurant, salon, clinic, or retail shop — the first-year figure is a different order entirely, frequently **several hundred thousand dirhams**, driven by fit-out, real rent paid in advance, equipment, stock, and a larger payroll.

These two shapes are illustrative, not quotes. Build your own from the lines above, verify each figure against the current position, and you will hold a budget you can stand behind. Profit, as Chapter 1 put it, is an opinion; cash is a fact. The ready-reckoner exists so that the fact does not surprise you.



PART THREE — RESOURCES

# Glossary of Dubai business terms

*The vocabulary of setting up — defined plainly, in one place.*

---

A founder meets a great deal of new vocabulary in the first weeks, much of it abbreviated, some of it used loosely even by people who should know better. This glossary defines the terms as they are used in this book and in practice. Where a term names an authority or a fee, treat the surrounding detail as indicative and subject to change, and confirm the current position through the official channel.

**Audit.** An independent examination of a company's financial statements. Required for some activities and by many free zones; confirm whether your jurisdiction and activity require one.

**Commercial licence.** A licence for trading activities — buying and selling goods. One of the main licence categories alongside professional and industrial.

**Corporate tax.** The UAE federal tax on business profits: 9% on profits above AED 375,000, and 0% below that threshold, for financial years starting on or after 1 June 2023. Administered by the FTA.

**Customs code.** A registration with customs that allows a company to import and export goods. Needed by trading and logistics businesses; obtained after the licence is issued.

**DET — Dubai Department of Economy and Tourism.** The authority that licenses mainland companies in Dubai, formerly known as the DED. Governs mainland trade names, activities, initial approvals, and licence renewals.

**DLD — Dubai Land Department.** The authority regulating real estate in Dubai. RERA operates within it.

**Ejari.** The system that registers a commercial or residential tenancy contract in Dubai. An Ejari-registered lease is required to use real premises as a licensed address.

**Establishment card.** The document, issued after the trade licence, that authorises a company to sponsor residence visas. Also called the immigration card. Nothing on the visa side can begin without it.

**Flexi-desk.** A shared or hot-desk package offered by many free zones that satisfies the requirement for a registered address without a private office. Common for lean service businesses and a low-cost entry point.

**Free zone.** A designated economic area with its own licensing authority, activity list, and rules. Dubai has 40-plus free zones. They permit 100% foreign ownership and suit businesses serving international or business clients.

**Free zone authority.** The body that governs a particular free zone — for example DMCC, IFZA, Meydan, or Dubai South. It issues licences, registers names, provides premises, and usually processes visas for companies in its zone.

**FTA — Federal Tax Authority.** The federal body administering VAT and corporate tax: registration, returns, and deregistration.

**GDRFA — General Directorate of Residency and Foreigners Affairs.** The authority governing entry permits, residence visas, and the residency status of those a company sponsors.

**Golden Visa.** A long-term UAE residence visa, typically valid for five or ten years, granted to qualifying investors, entrepreneurs, skilled professionals, and others. Eligibility criteria change; confirm the current rules.

**Immigration card.** Another name for the establishment card — the document that lets a company sponsor visas.

**Industrial licence.** A licence for manufacturing and industrial activities — processing, assembly, or production of goods.

**Initial approval.** An authority's confirmation, in principle, that it has no objection to a proposed activity and the proposed owners. A milestone early in setup; it is not the licence itself.

**KHDA — Knowledge and Human Development Authority.** The authority governing private education and training in Dubai — schools, nurseries, and training and tutoring centres.

**Mainland.** A company licensed by DET that may trade directly anywhere in the UAE, including to the public and government. 100% foreign ownership is permitted for the large majority of activities since the 2021 reform.

**MoA — Memorandum of Association.** The constitutional document of a company, setting out its shareholders, share structure, and governance. Free zones use this or an equivalent document.

**MOHRE — Ministry of Human Resources and Emiratisation.** The federal ministry governing labour and employment for mainland companies — contracts, work permits, the WPS, and Emiratisation.

**NOC — No-Objection Certificate.** A formal letter from a relevant party — an employer, a sponsor, or an authority — stating it has no objection to a proposed action, such as a person setting up or joining a company.

**Offshore.** A company used purely for holding, intellectual property, and international structuring — for example JAFZA Offshore or RAK ICC. It has no UAE operating presence and grants no residence visas.

**PRO — Public Relations Officer.** A person, often engaged through a service firm, who handles government paperwork — licensing, visas, and approvals — on a company's behalf. A common way for founders to navigate procedures efficiently.

**Professional licence.** A licence for service-based activities that rely on intellectual or professional skill — consultancy, marketing, IT services, and similar.

**Qualifying free zone person.** A free zone company that, by meeting specific conditions, may retain a 0% corporate tax rate on its qualifying income. The conditions are detailed and have evolved; confirm the current rules with the FTA.

**RERA — Real Estate Regulatory Agency.** The agency within the DLD that regulates the real estate profession — brokerage, broker permits and examinations, and property management.

**RTA — Roads and Transport Authority.** The Dubai authority governing vehicles, transport, and mobility permits — relevant to car rental, chauffeur services, and delivery operations. Salik sits under its wider remit.

**Salik.** Dubai's automated road-toll system. A charge is deducted electronically each time a vehicle passes a toll gate — a running cost for any vehicle-based business.

**Tasheel.** The MOHRE service channel through which much mainland labour and employment paperwork — contracts and work permits — is processed.

**Tourism Dirham.** A levy applied to guest stays in hotels and holiday homes, collected per room per night. Relevant to hospitality and holiday-homes businesses.

**Trade name.** The registered commercial name of a company. It must meet the authority's naming rules and is reserved early in setup, before or alongside initial approval.

**Trakheesi.** DET's system for processing many mainland permits, approvals, and licensing-related transactions in Dubai.

**VAT — Value Added Tax.** A consumption tax of 5%, in force in the UAE since 2018. Registration with the FTA is mandatory once taxable supplies meet the threshold, and optional below it. Confirm the current thresholds with the FTA.

**Working capital.** The cash that funds day-to-day operations — stock, salaries, rent, marketing — before a business reliably pays for itself. Not a setup fee; the fuel that carries the business through its runway.

**WPS — Wage Protection System.** The UAE system through which employers pay salaries electronically so that wages are paid in full and on time. Required for mainland employers.



PART THREE — RESOURCES

# A final word

*The send-off — what to carry out of this book and into the city.*

---

You have reached the end of a long book, and it is worth being plain about what it was and was not designed to do. It was not designed to convince you to start a business in Dubai. It was designed to let you decide, with the facts in front of you, whether you should — and then, if you should, to put the work in an order that does not double back on itself. If you have read honestly, you now know things about your own plan that a promotional video would never have told you. That knowledge is the most valuable thing you can carry out of these pages.

Return, for a moment, to where Chapter 1 began. Dubai is not a lottery ticket, and it was never built to be one. It is a well-engineered marketplace that pays out, reliably, to people who arrive prepared. Every chapter since has been in the service of that single word. The jurisdiction decision, the licence sequence, the cash cycle, the thirty-five industries, the directories and the ready-reckoner in this part — none of it was filler. All of it was preparation. The founder who has worked through this book is not guaranteed success; no one can offer that. But that founder has removed the avoidable failures, and in a fast, crowded, properly regulated market, removing the avoidable failures is most of the contest.

Three ideas from Chapter 1 deserve to be the last things you hear, because they will serve you long after the setup is finished.

The first: **profit is an opinion; cash is a fact.** A spreadsheet can show a profit while the bank account empties — through rent paid a year in advance, through receivables that take ninety days to arrive, through a launch that spent everything on the licence and the fit-out and left nothing for the months that matter. Watch the fact. A business that manages its cash buys itself the time to get everything else right. A business that does not will run out of time before it runs out of ideas.

The second: **the licence is the start of the work, not the proof of it.** Setup is the most certain part of the journey, which is exactly why it tempts founders to over-invest in it and mistake it for an achievement. The day your licence is issued, you have not built a business. You have earned the right to begin. The real work — the first three customers, the disciplined renewals, the slow accumulation of a reputation — starts the morning after, and it is longer and quieter than the launch.

The third, and the one to hold longest: **Dubai rewards the prepared.** It is genuinely on the side of the operator who arrives with a real runway, a service the city wants, the temperament for a competitive market, and the patience to do the unglamorous work for longer than feels comfortable. That is not a slogan. It is the most reliable observation in this book, repeated across thirty-five industries because it held true in every one of them.

So here is the honest send-off. If you are not ready — if the runway is three months and the business is a twelve-month business, if the demand has been imagined rather than tested, if the plan still rests on the word "tax-free" — then the kindest thing this book can tell you is *not yet*. Note the words. Not *no*: *not yet*. Extend the runway. Validate the demand. Correct the expectation. The city is not going anywhere, and it will be a far better bet for the founder who arrives in the right order.

And if you are ready — if you have done the work these pages asked of you, assembled the budget, chosen the jurisdiction for the customer you are actually building for, and you can name where your first three customers will come from — then there is nothing left to do but begin. Begin carefully, begin with your cash protected and your runway honest, and begin knowing that the licence in your hand is a starting line and not a finishing one.

Dubai was built, deliberately, by people who arrived from somewhere else and decided to make something. It still works that way. It still has room for the next one who arrives prepared, does the work, and stays long enough to be good at it.

That could be you. Go and do it properly.
